    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer18374531291941173159">No one description will fit this strange, eccentric, endlessly complex masterpiece. It is a fiction about fiction-writing in which the invented world is as much infused with wit and genius as the theme of inventing it. It is a joyful celebration of the infinite possibilities of the art of fiction, and a wry demonstration of its limitations. This Penguin Classic contains Ch</span>
  <span id="freeText18374531291941173159" style="display:none">No one description will fit this strange, eccentric, endlessly complex masterpiece. It is a fiction about fiction-writing in which the invented world is as much infused with wit and genius as the theme of inventing it. It is a joyful celebration of the infinite possibilities of the art of fiction, and a wry demonstration of its limitations. This Penguin Classic contains Christopher Ricks's introductory essay, itself a classic of English literary criticism, together with a new introduction on the recent critical history and influence of Tristram Shandy by Melvyn New. The text and notes are based on the acclaimed Florida Edition, making the scholarship of the Florida editors readily available for the first time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18374531291941173159" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      $('edition_40236755').observe('prototip:shown', function() {
        if (this.up('#box')) {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});

      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('edition_40236755').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('edition_40236755').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('edition_40236755').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780679405603" href="/book/show/777379.Tristram_Shandy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=1001-books-to-read-before-you-die">1001-books-to-read-before-you-die</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=monster-mash-of-a-mess">monster-mash-of-a-mess</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=havanas-en-masse">havanas-en-masse</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=unforgettable">unforgettable</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2065330860" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5789798641390420550">Before I start my review of this delightful classic, I have to tell you a short anecdote from my teaching life. But don’t worry, it is not really a digression at all, as it is leading directly to the essence of this novel. It actually has more relevance for Tristram Shandy than many of the anecdotes Tristram himself tells in his story. If it is a digression, (which I formally dispute, partly because you can’t really digress before you have begun, and partly because it is crucial for the review’s</span>
  <span id="freeText5789798641390420550" style="display:none">Before I start my review of this delightful classic, I have to tell you a short anecdote from my teaching life. But don’t worry, it is not really a digression at all, as it is leading directly to the essence of this novel. It actually has more relevance for Tristram Shandy than many of the anecdotes Tristram himself tells in his story. If it is a digression, (which I formally dispute, partly because you can’t really digress before you have begun, and partly because it is crucial for the review’s essential development), BUT IF it should be considered a digression (by the harsh standards of formal review guidelines and rules), it certainly is of the noble Tristram-kind known as a “progressive digression”.<br /> <br />It is also quite modest and unpretentious, as it won’t need any footnotes, and it won’t come with Latin quotes either, or with omitted or ripped out chapters. It will simply be a short introductory tale - setting the stage for the review to come. <br /> <br /><br />Here it is then, without any further announcement!<br /><br />Anecdote leading to the formal beginning of the review:<br /><br />I used to teach a very peculiar class for a couple of years. They were known throughout the school for their lively interest in everything and for their almost inexhaustible talent for digression. Whatever you set out to teach them, they took over and formed a lesson of their own according to their curiosity and enthusiasm. You had to prepare for their classes in exactly the opposite way compared to all other groups. In other lessons, you were trying your best to stimulate interest and to engage in interactive discussions to keep the students remotely awake, but with this set of adolescents you had to plan some deliberately, excruciatingly boring elements in order to curb their energies, and to guide them towards some kind of focus. They had so many questions to ask, so many anecdotes to tell, so many viewpoints to argue, that you simply did not get to finish a single chapter in the history book on time. That, of course, is inconvenient as you can’t postpone the assessment of the Grade 8 curriculum to Grade 10.<br /><br />One day, when I was particularly tired - it was the last period in the afternoon - I lost control of their discussion. Whoever has taught a lively class knows what I am talking about. You realise all of a sudden that you are completely off topic, that there are centuries of history to wade through to get back to the starting point, and that the class machine is running full speed towards the edge of reason. All hands were up, everyone wanted to share opinions and life stories, and I wanted to wrap up and go home. What to do? Slowly, steadily I started to take over the conductor job again, to guide the diverse contributions towards my goal, to rein in the cacophony of voices. We were just about to reestablish order and to close the chapter of the initial digression that had got the unruly crowd started, when one boy raised his hand and threw in another random thought, pointing straight towards new chaos. I finally lost my only superficially kept temper and yelled:<br /><br />“STOP DIGRESSING FROM THE DIGRESSION!”<br /><br />From then on, that became a standard saying in the class, a sure card to play to get them to laugh. <br /><br />Little did I know that they were complete amateurs, compared to the master Tristram Shandy!<br /><br />While my class just managed to make the analysis of the effects of crop rotation in the Industrial Revolution turn into something as closely related as revolutionary pop songs in the 21st century, Tristram manages to fill 8.5 hours of audiobook time to get born, while eagerly discussing his own nose, noses in general, his Uncle Toby, and the different dogmas of Protestants and Catholics, and several other important topics, including his name and the line of beauty and Don Quixote etc. etc. etc. (and I promise you that those enigmatic “etc” fill several hundred pages!).<br /><br />He accurately calculates that he won’t be able to finish the account of his life and opinions, as he is spending so much time on a couple of hours that he consistently accumulates years of backlog in his narrative. It runs in the family, as his father set out to write a pedagogical work for him, the Tristopaedia, which never caught up with the growing boy. <br /><br />While we glimpse quite a lot of Tristram’s family, their lives and their opinions, he is rather mum about his own person, always finding more important topics to talk about. Closing the novel, I know more about the mortality of Trim’s hat and about the amours of Uncle Toby than about Tristram himself. But that doesn’t really matter, for most of all, I know that the modern novel has some work to do to catch up with this experimental classic. <br /><br />What a pure joy to see the narrator tear the body of the novel open and show the scaffold of it in its artificial randomness. And what additional spice to get bits and pieces of Tristram’s erudition, wit, and sense of humour. Who needs a plot, anyway? Isn’t that more artificial in the end than a long dialogue on the pleasures and pains of dividing a work into chapters?<br /><br />Do I really need to know the details of a love story when the essence of love is rendered in alphabetical order instead?<br /><br />“Love is certainly, at least alphabetically speaking, one of the most<br />Agitating<br />Bewitching<br />Confounded<br />Devilish affairs of life - the most<br />Extravagant<br />Futilitous<br />Galligaskinish<br />Handy-dandyish<br />Iracundulous (there is no K in it) and<br />Lyrical of all human passions: at the same time, the most<br />Misgiving<br />Ninnyhammering<br />Obstipating<br />Pragmatical<br />Stridulous<br />Ridiculous - though by the bye the R should have gone first”<br /><br />As you can imagine, I could go on and on, from one thread to another, and still not be any closer to starting my review, so I will make a drastic decision, and urge you to let Tristram speak for himself instead - there is no one like him to speak anyway. <br /><br />Please read his digressions! <br /><br />They are much more amusing than I can adequately show you. I strongly recommend the audio version, as it forced me to sit still and not digress from the text in the way I might have otherwise, had I had the slightest chance. I recommend having a copy of the book next to you as well, as some pages are more interesting in a visual than in an auditive, not to mention narrative, way. <br /><br />To keep seated, I employed my hands with yarn as well, spinning my own threads into a warm poncho which will come in very handy when the teaching season starts again, - as will my time with Tristram, for I can’t imagine any lecture that could possibly prepare you better for the digressions of students than the life and opinions of Tristram Shandy!<br /><br />A superb experiment of a novel, and a unique voice in world literature! <br /><br />As for my review, I accidentally ripped it out of my Goodreads account and replaced it with this text instead. Sometimes things like that happen, and the original review is in the literary ether together with the missing chapter in Tristram Shandy. To tell the story of all those alternative texts, we would need the help of Borges and his Labyrinths. But that is another story - or two, or three...</span>
  <a data-text-id="5789798641390420550" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

<span id="freeTextContainer10060791515830138527">Before I began on this book----<br/><br/>Wait a moment, don’t climb on your hobby-horse, or rather, don’t pounce on your keyboard to tell me that I didn’t begin this book, that it was Laurence Sterne who began this book more than two hundred and fifty years ago, long before I was even a * in my mothers’s eye or an answering * in my father’s----<br/><br/>So, before I began reading this book, like many amongst you, I had preconceived ideas about what----<br/><br/>Yes, it is worth paying attention to the wording here because th</span>
  <span id="freeText10060791515830138527" style="display:none">Before I began on this book----<br /><br />Wait a moment, don’t climb on your hobby-horse, or rather, don’t pounce on your keyboard to tell me that I didn’t begin this book, that it was Laurence Sterne who began this book more than two hundred and fifty years ago, long before I was even a * in my mothers’s eye or an answering * in my father’s----<br /><br />So, before I began reading this book, like many amongst you, I had preconceived ideas about what----<br /><br />Yes, it is worth paying attention to the wording here because the <i>Life and</i> some of <i>the Opinions of Tristram Shandy</i> relate to a time even before the moment of his own ✹----<br /><br />But the <i>Life and Opinions</i> is still a good title, and the Opinions themselves, dating as they do from the 1760s, must have been preserved in the finest liquor to have retained such a freshness of spirit that you would think they had taken their first breath of Life a mere five minutes before----<br /><br />Did I mention Time?<br /><br />So yes, before I began reading Sterne, I had a sort of bias against this innocent book----<br /><br />No, not innocent as to what happens between its covers, no, for it treats of everything in the world and doesn’t mince its words in the telling, doesn’t strut about like a turkey cock but rather talks turkey, as in gets straight to the----<br /><br />But to go back to where I began, that period of time before I picked up Shandy and conceived the fancy----<br /><br />No, not that kind of fancy. Why is it that we humans are ever occupied by conceptions of a double-meaning nature, as if words were not already weighty enough without adding----<br /><br />Although generally, at least today, few of us seem capable of constructing sentences fortified with the kind of ravelins or outworks favoured by Tristram’s uncle Toby and his trusty henchman Trim. For you must accept, if you are to read Sterne that the work is a very well fortified construction, that every sentence contains at least two facets or aspects, and each aspect faces off at an angle as in goes in a different direction and therefore the time taken to read----<br /><br />Did I mention Time, because the notion, the very conception of Time is very central to any coherent understanding of the relationship between the writing of this book and the reading of it----<br /><br />And speaking of the reading, we mustn't underestimate the importance of the Opinions of the reader; although not mentioned in the title, the Opinions of the reader are nevertheless----<br /><br />The narrative, such as is, is interrupted by frequent digressions directed at the reader and when these asides are aimed in particular at the female reader, well----<br /><br />Broadsides are not out of place to mention, in fact the terminology of the battlefield is used for even the most peaceful-sounding conversations, not to speak of matters amatory----<br /><br />And although seemingly random, the trajectory of the narrative is very precisely plotted between order and chaos, between sense and nonsense, between mysteries and riddles, between the spiritual and the natural, between abstract philosophy and practical wisdom, between noses and----<br /><br />Since the writer, together with his appendages, is always present in his own writing, just as every man is present at the shaving of his own beard, Laurence Sterne may also be----<br /><br />We can’t but speculate that the subacid personality Sterne gives Tristram’s father, a man who is pedantically obtuse and razor-sharp at the same time, and the ridiculously cautious diplomacy he allows Tristram’s mother (though it is a clever and perfectly impenetrable protection against the father’s razor wit, and blunts it nicely from time to time), plus the childlike humanity with which he endows Tristram's uncle Toby----<br /><br />However, it is the character of the parson Yorick, who, like his namesake in Hamlet, that fellow of such ‘infinite jest’, has a great appreciation of nonsense which he allies with a paradoxical impatience of folly and verbosity, and the whole may give us the truest picture of Laurence----<br /><br />I’m reminded of Joyce’s 'Man in the Macintosh', the ghost-like figure who flits in and out of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/338798.Ulysses" title="Ulysses by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Ulysses</a>, and aren’t there many such elusive raincoated men in Beckett too----<br /><br />So yes, I think Sterne may well have inserted himself into his own novel via Yorick, his Jester of a parson----<br /><br />But for this speculation, I have no proof, at least ready, so I will_that point unless some hypercritick reader of this review wants to ↵ to it----<br /><br />The last word I will allow to Sterne:<br /><i>Therefore, my dear friend and companion (reader), if you should think me somewhat sparing of my narrative on my first setting out,---bear with me,----and let me go on, and tell my story my own way:---or if I should seem now and then to trifle upon the road,--or should sometimes put on a fool’s cap with a bell to it, for a moment or two as we pass along,--don’t fly off,--but rather courteously give me credit for a little more wisdom than appears upon my outside;-----and as we jog on, either laugh with me, or at me, or in short, do any thing,-----only keep your temper.</i> </span>

    <a title="Renato Magalhães Rocha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha"><img alt="Renato Magalhães Rocha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1524097330p2/29402621.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/920434199?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Renato Magalhães Rocha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Renato Magalhães Rocha" href="/user/show/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha">Renato Magalhães Rocha</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788571648302" href="/book/show/21964663-a-vida-e-as-opini-es-do-cavalheiro-tristram-shandy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=the-novel-100">the-novel-100</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=100-best-books-world-library">100-best-books-world-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=absolute-favorites">absolute-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=all-stars">all-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=1001">1001</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer920434199" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12125139716177540412">I failed big time in reviewing this.<br/>Oh well.<br/>I tried mentioning Sterne's style and his humor. I tried to include some of my favorite quotes and even show one of the cool drawings included. And I tried stating how much I loved it.<br/>However, when I finished and read it, it didn't do the book any justice at all.<br/>So all that's left for me to do is tell you to go read it.<br/><br/>Rating: 5 stars<br/><br/><s>This is one of those books we encounter in life that, despite being completely enchanted and raptured chapter after chap</s></span>
  <span id="freeText12125139716177540412" style="display:none">I failed big time in reviewing this.<br>Oh well.<br>I tried mentioning Sterne's style and his humor. I tried to include some of my favorite quotes and even show one of the cool drawings included. And I tried stating how much I loved it.<br>However, when I finished and read it, it didn't do the book any justice at all.<br>So all that's left for me to do is tell you to go read it.<br><br>Rating: 5 stars<br><br><s>This is one of those books we encounter in life that, despite being completely enchanted and raptured chapter after chapter, yet we wonder whether it's even possible to properly communicate these feelings to your fellow book addicts using only words. You, who is now reading this tentative review and who, unfortunately, have not yet heard much about <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/76527.The_Life_and_Opinions_of_Tristram_Shandy__Gentleman" title="The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman by Laurence Sterne" rel="nofollow noopener">The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman</a>, might about now be thinking that this is a profound, piercing read, that by making use of deep and emotional passages amazes us with its insights on life. If that's what you looking for in a book, you won't get it here. This is comedy!<br><br>Heavily influenced by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3836.Don_Quixote" title="Don Quixote by Miguel de Cervantes Saavedra" rel="nofollow noopener">Don Quixote</a> and Cervantes and his humor, lightness and stand-alone stories, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/22796.Laurence_Sterne" title="Laurence Sterne" rel="nofollow noopener">Laurence Sterne</a> was still able to be original and create a masterpiece of his own, taking the humor he found in the stories of the knight-errant and his squire to a whole new level, gracefully and, I must say, obscenely–although he was careful enough to thinly disguise it all in metaphors and many ******–not that it avoided him any criticism. While so influenced by another work, <i>Tristram Shandy</i> is still highly original and consequently inspired itself many writers with his style. Some believe Sterne to have been the forerunner of many narrative devices used by authors such as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5144.James_Joyce" title="James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">James Joyce</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6765.Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf" rel="nofollow noopener">Virginia Woolf</a>. Speaking of great writers, I should add that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/128382.Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy" rel="nofollow noopener">Leo Tolstoy</a> named Sterne his favorite author.<br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/76527.The_Life_and_Opinions_of_Tristram_Shandy__Gentleman" title="The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman by Laurence Sterne" rel="nofollow noopener">The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman</a> was released in nine volumes throughout seven years (Vols. 1 &amp; 2 in 1759; Vols. 3 &amp; 4 in 1761; Vols. 5 &amp; 6 in 1762; Vols. 7 &amp; 8 in 1765; and finally, Vol. 9 in 1767). Luckily for us it is now just one volume divided by its chapters and subchapters. The book, as its title suggests, aims to tell the story of Tristram Shandy–he is writing his own biography. Our Tristram, however, can't tell a story from beginning to end even if it meant to save his life. He feels he has to go back in order to give us plenty of details and his own birth only happens in Vol. 3. From that you can get an idea of what you're getting yourself into!<br><br><blockquote>
<i>"What these perplexities of my uncle </i>Toby<i> were,——’tis impossible for you to guess;—if you could,—I should blush; not as a relation,—not as a man,—nor even as a woman,—but I should blush as an author; inasmuch as I set no small store by myself upon this very account, that my reader has never yet been able to guess at anything. And in this, Sir, I am of so nice and singular a humour, that if I thought you was able to form the least judgment or probable conjecture to yourself, of what was to come in the next page,—I would tear it out of my book.."</i>
</blockquote>
<br>Sterne himself, in the quote above, summarized perfectly what one should expect to find in his book–or how one shouldn't expect anything as it's very unlikely that his inventive mind would be matched. Mightily original in the events depicted on the book, he takes it even further with many different design features and digressions. In the drawing below (by the writer himself), he explains his narrative course in the first four volumes, swearing by us that the fifth would be finer - but would you take his word for it?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1423956110i/13694201.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><blockquote><i>"Digressions, incontestably, are the sunshine;——they are the life, the soul of reading!—take them out of this book, for instance,—you might as well take the book along with them;—one cold eternal winter would reign in every page of it; restore them to the writer;—he steps forth like a bridegroom,—bids All-hail; brings in variety, and forbids the appetite to fail."</i></blockquote>
<br>Speaking of digressions, while it might be extremely irritating for many who hoped for chronological progress and a steady narrative, to my taste, it was the highlight of this experience. Not only because it was a lot of fun, but also because it felt close to home as it seemed like the way my grandmother tells her stories: even the simplest event as <i>"I didn't sleep well at all last night"</i> comes with the physical description of her neighboor who also has trouble sleeping who has a brother that's a pharmacist (insert his background life story here) and his third degree relations with our family and how tonight she'll finally sleep as she'll not forget to take her pills–which was the reason of her insomnia.<br><br>By digressing so much, Tristram is merely following his train of thought, without making any effort to order those ideas for his reader's convenience. Isn't that a simpler version of the stream of consciouness technique so hailed in the next centuries? While the former device follows wherever the narrator mind takes him, but still describing the events in a logical order, the latter strips more layers and simply exposes the very thought in a loose manner.<br><br><blockquote><i>"You must have a little patience. I have undertaken, you see, to write not only my life, but my opinions also (...) if you should think me somewhat sparing of my narrative on my first setting out—bear with me,—and let me go on, and tell my story my own way:—Or, if I should seem now and then to trifle upon the road,—or should sometimes put on a fool’s cap with a bell to it, for a moment or two as we pass along,—don’t fly off,—but rather courteously 9 give me credit for a little more wisdom than appears upon my outside;—and as we jog on, either laugh with me, or at me, or in short, do anything,—only keep your temper."</i></blockquote>
<br>So, I urge you: do have patience with him. He's worth it!<br><br>Film adaptation: well, what do you know? While I was reading the book I lamented that it was impossible to be filmed. Turns out it wasn't. <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0423409/" rel="nofollow noopener">A Cock and a Bull Story</a> is a film-within-a-film released in 2005 by bold director Michael Winterbottom that tells the story of two actors (Steve Coogan and Rob Byrdon, playing themselves) who are shooting an adaptation of Sterne’s novel, but the film (<i>Tristram</i>) fails terribly–just as the book is about a man attempting to write his autobiography but never really succeeding while at it for he can’t properly tell his story without being distracted, the film is about an attempt at making a film. Winterbottom was happy in the majority of his choices and unlike many film adaptations I’ve watched, this one actually works.<br><br>Review: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/76527.The_Life_and_Opinions_of_Tristram_Shandy__Gentleman" title="The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman by Laurence Sterne" rel="nofollow noopener">The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman</a> is one of those books I had heard about but never planned to actually read any time soon. Luckily, I was tempted by a group read and found a copy of a rare Portuguese translation (it's been out of print in Brazil for years) at the last minute. It's now absolutely one of my favorites: 5 very assymetric stars.</s></span>
  <a data-text-id="12125139716177540412" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12921109203413889500">So many great discoveries were made absolutely unintentionally…<br/><b>Christopher Columbus</b> was sailing to India and unexpectedly discovered America without any slightest suspicions.<br/><b>Laurence Sterne</b> was writing some obscure petty biography and unawares discovered postmodernism.<br/>But the most weird and paradoxical thing about it is that he discovered postmodernism long before the modernists managed to discover modernism.<br/><blockquote>It had ever been the custom of the family, and by length of time was almost become a mat</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText12921109203413889500" style="display:none">So many great discoveries were made absolutely unintentionally…<br /><b>Christopher Columbus</b> was sailing to India and unexpectedly discovered America without any slightest suspicions.<br /><b>Laurence Sterne</b> was writing some obscure petty biography and unawares discovered postmodernism.<br />But the most weird and paradoxical thing about it is that he discovered postmodernism long before the modernists managed to discover modernism.<br /><blockquote>It had ever been the custom of the family, and by length of time was almost become a matter of common right, that the eldest son of it should have free ingress, egress, and regress into foreign parts before marriage, – not only for the sake of bettering his own private parts, by the benefit of exercise and change of so much air – but simply for the mere delectation of his fancy, by the feather put into his cap, of having been abroad.</blockquote><br />And at that he was a clergyman. Strange are your deeds, Oh Lord.<br />But the most important thing is that God dictated to <b>Laurence Sterne</b> a universal postmodern rule: ‘never piss out of your window’.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer127075724" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer4031845721424972382">963.	The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman = Tristram Shandy, Laurence Sterne<br/>Tristram Shandy is a novel by Laurence Sterne. It was published in nine volumes, the first two appearing in 1759, and seven others following over the next seven years (vols. 3 and 4, 1761; vols. 5 and 6, 1762; vols. 7 and 8, 1765; vol. 9, 1767). <br/>As its title suggests, the book is ostensibly Tristram's narration of his life story. But it is one of the central jokes of the novel that he cannot explain anythi</span>
  <span id="freeText4031845721424972382" style="display:none">963.	The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman = Tristram Shandy, Laurence Sterne<br />Tristram Shandy is a novel by Laurence Sterne. It was published in nine volumes, the first two appearing in 1759, and seven others following over the next seven years (vols. 3 and 4, 1761; vols. 5 and 6, 1762; vols. 7 and 8, 1765; vol. 9, 1767). <br />As its title suggests, the book is ostensibly Tristram's narration of his life story. But it is one of the central jokes of the novel that he cannot explain anything simply, that he must make explanatory diversions to add context and color to his tale, to the extent that Tristram's own birth is not even reached until Volume III. ... :)<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز بیست و پنجم ماه سپتامبر سال 2000 میلادی<br />عنوان: زندگانی و عقاید آقای تریسترام شندی؛ نویسنده: لارنس استرن؛ مترجم: ابراهیم یونسی؛ انتشارات نسل قلم، 1378، در دو جلد؛ <br /><br />رمانی‌ ست نوشته ی «لارنس استرن»، در نه جلد (نسخه اصلی). دو جلد نخست از رمان در سال 1759 میلادی منتشر شد، و جلدهای بعدی طی هفت سال پی آیند به چاپ رسیدند. کتاب «داستان زندگی و عقاید» نجیب‌زاده‌ ای به نام «تریسترام شندی»ست. نویسنده برای نوشتن این رمان شیوه‌ ای را به کار می‌گیرد، که ادامه ی سنت «فرانسوا رابله»، در داستان‌گویی‌ است. داستان «تریسترام شندی» پر است از، اظهار نظرها، و عقاید راوی، که به شکلی نامنظم، و پراکنده، به خوانشگر ارائه می‌شوند، و نویسنده با کلک‌بازی‌های فراوان، بازگویی اصل داستان را، مرتب به تأخیر می‌اندازد. این شیوه بعدها در آثار «جیمز جویس»، نویسنده ی ایرلندی، و «دیوید فاستر والاس» نویسنده ی رمان و داستان‌های کوتاه آمریکایی، نیز، ادامه یافته است. «زندگی و عقاید آقای تریسترام شندی» را، جناب آقای «ابراهیم یونسی» به فارسی ترجمه کرده، و انتشارت نسل قلم در سال 1378 هجری خورشیدی آن را در دو جلد منتشر کرده‌ است<br />نقل از نمونه متن: ای کاش پدر یا مادرم، یا در واقع هردو …؛ چون هردو موظف به این کار بودند …؛ وقتی مرا به وجود میآوردند، میدانستند چه میکنند، اگر چنانکه باید، به این امر توجه میکردند، و میدیدند که چه چیزها به این کارشان بستگی دارد، و نه تنها پای به وجود آوردن یک موجود معقول، در میان است، بلکه، مسئله ی تشکیل و تشکل مناسب حرارت بدن این موجود، و احتمالاً نبوغ و ساختمان مغز او هم، مطرح است، و حتی ممکن است، سرنوشت همه ی خاندان این موجود، از اخلاط و امیالی تاثیر پذیرد، که آن هنگام غلبه داشتند ...؛ اگر به واسطه ی آن دو اسب سركش، و آن راننده ی دیوانه نبود، که ما را از «استیلتن» به «استامفورد» برد، این فکر هرگز به ذهنم راه نمییافت. مثل برق میرفت، یک سراشیب سه میل و نیمی بود، که طی آن به زحمت اگر پایمان با زمین تماس مییافت …؛ بس که آهنگ حرکتمان سریع بود ...؛ خلاصه، فکر به مغز آمد، و قلب هم در آن مشاركت كرد . پایان نقل از نمونه. ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="4031845721424972382" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_6901362" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6901362" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6901362?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6901362" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15307985205912593875"><br/>I would like to dedicate the following old review to a much missed GR friend, Bird Brian, who appears as a character in my review. He provided us with many hours of free entertainment with his great rants against every possible aspect of capitalism and the American government. But 50% of him left when Amazon bought GR, and the rest of him disappeared when the censorship controversy splurged all over our heads. And now he is not here to excoriate all the bad people and discover all the conspiraci</span>
  <span id="freeText15307985205912593875" style="display:none"><br>I would like to dedicate the following old review to a much missed GR friend, Bird Brian, who appears as a character in my review. He provided us with many hours of free entertainment with his great rants against every possible aspect of capitalism and the American government. But 50% of him left when Amazon bought GR, and the rest of him disappeared when the censorship controversy splurged all over our heads. And now he is not here to excoriate all the bad people and discover all the conspiracies. <br><br>****<br><br><br>NICHOLAS PARSONS: Welcome to "Just A Minute!" <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380302503i/687645.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>THEME MUSIC <br><br>NP: Thank you, thank you, hello, my name is Nicholas Parsons. And as the Minute Waltz fades away once more it is my pleasure to welcome our many listeners, not only in this country but throughout the world. But also to welcome to the show this week four highly talented and individual players of this game. And once again they're going to show their invention, their verbal dexterity and their creative ingenuity as they speak on a subject that I give them for one minute, and they try and do that without hesitation, repetition or deviation. And this week our four contestants are Manny Rayner, Bird Brian, Paul Bryant and Ian Graye. Please welcome all four of them! (Applause). And we start this week with Manny Rayner – Manny, the subject is Tristram Shandy. Tell us something about that Manny, without hesitation, repetition or deviation starting now. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380302503i/687646.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>MANNY : This is a novel written between 1759 and 1765 –<br><br>BUZZZ!<br><br>NP : And Bird Brian has challenged. What is your challenge?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380302503i/687647.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br>BB : Repetition of “seventeen”.<br><br>MR : But that’s part of the designation of the year… 1953, 1954…<br><br>NP : A harsh challenge but I’m going to have to agree with Brian – so BB you have a point and you have the subject of Tristram Shandy and there are 56 seconds left.<br><br>BB : Ironically, given that Tristram Shandy is the epitome of deviation and digression, we here are supposed to discuss it without ourselves digressing – if I remember rightly it has been filmed as A Cock and Bull Story which was directed by Michael Winterbottom who also did Welcome to Sarajevo –<br><br>BUZZ!<br><br>NP : And Paul Bryant has challenged.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380302503i/687648.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br>PB : Well, it was all getting so terribly dull I thought I’d press this buzzer just to wake us up again.<br><br>NP : But what is your challenge? Dullness is allowed in this panel game.<br><br>PB : Well… he deviated by going on about Sarajevo. I could see he was just trying to drag politics into it again.<br><br>NP : Well no, he only mentioned one other film, I don’t think that was really deviating from the subject. So Brian you have a point for an incorrect challenge and you continue with Tristram Shandy with 22 seconds left.<br><br>BB : Er –<br><br>BUZZ!<br><br>Ian Graye : Hesitation.<br><br><img src="https://s3.amazonaws.com/photo.goodreads.com/users/1298537744p2/5022264.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br>NP : Oh definitely. You have to keep going in this game, loquacity is the thing. So Ian you have 21 seconds left with Tristram Shandy.<br><br>IG : This has got to be one of the most brilliant, funniest and –<br><br>Buzz!<br><br>NP : Er – who challenged there? Manny?<br><br>MR : Deviation. I can’t understand his accent.<br><br>NP: What?<br><br>MR : He could be talking about anything , how would we know.<br><br>NP : Well, er, he does have an Australian accent, of course, but I thought he was perfectly comprehensible… let’s ask the audience. Audience – can you understand Ian Graye?<br><br>Audience : Mooo!<br><br>PB : It’s hopeless asking that lot, they’re just a lot of sheep.<br><br>Audience : Moo! Mooo!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380302503i/687649.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br>NP : So Ian that was a wrong challenge, you have a point and the subject is back with you, 19 seconds left for Tristram Shandy.<br><br>Ian : Here is a novel that parodies many of the cliches of later novelists before they became – <br><br>Buzz!<br><br>PB : Repetition.<br><br>NP : Repetition?<br><br>PB : Yes, repetition of “novel”.<br><br>Ian : No, I said “novel” and novel<b>ist</b>” – two different words, like “wood” and “tree”, or is your dictionary different to mine?<br><br>NP : Yes, he did you know.<br><br>BB : Quite so.<br><br>NP : So, another point for another wrong challenge and you have the subject back, Ian, 13 seconds starting now.<br><br>IG : When I was –<br><br>Buzz!<br><br>MR : Deviation – he’s talking about himself now, not Tristram Shandy.<br><br>NP : A very clever challenge! So you get a point for that and the subject back with you, 11 seconds for Tristram Shandy.<br><br>MR : The full title is The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman, which gives Lawrence Sterne ample leeway to throw a in lot of rabbiting about anything. I haven’t actually read this book –<br><br>Buzz!<br><br>NP : And Paul Bryant has challenged.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380302503i/687650._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br>PB : Deviation – if he hasn’t read it he can’t say anything about it and ought to leave it to those of us who have. <br><br>NP : Well that’s er frankly ridiculous, I haven’t climbed Mount Everest but I can talk about it, I know facts about Mount Everest. <br><br>PB : Now you’re deviating. What’s Mount Everest got to do with it?<br><br>NP : But I’m the chairman, I’m allowed to repeat and hesitate and deviate. I relish my deviant status.<br><br>PB : You’re in cahoots with him! – Infamy, infamy, they’ve all got it infamy!<br><br>NP : Be that as it may, the subject is back with Manny and there are only three seconds left starting now.<br><br>Manny : I fully intend to read this wonderful volume at the earliest –<br><br>WHISTLE<br><br>NP : And the sound of the whistle beautifully blown by our producer Samantha indicates the end of that – strenuous – round. Manny gets a point for speaking as the whistle went, and I can now reveal that the situation is that he is our joint leader with Ian, Bird Brian is next, and Paul Bryant yet to score. So we begin Round Two and the subject is Why I Am So Grumpy and Paul, you can begin with that, starting now.<br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="15307985205912593875" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_691208851" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis"><img alt="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548295774p2/7607437.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/691208851?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" href="/user/show/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis">Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/6957335-the-life-and-opinions-of-tristram-shandy-gentleman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=moore-novelnovel">moore-novelnovel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=eighteen-centuries">eighteen-centuries</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=encyclopedic">encyclopedic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=unreadababble">unreadababble</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer691208851" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7280071174583046832"><b>The Shandian Spawn</b><br/><br/>“If on a friend’s bookshelf<br/> You cannot find Joyce or Sterne<br/> Cervantes, Rabelais, or Burton,<br/><br/> “[Gaddis or Gass, Pynchon or McElroy,<br/> David Foster Wallace, William T Vollmann,<br/> Alexander Theroux or Gilbert Sorrentino,]<br/><br/> “You are in danger, face the fact,<br/> So kick him first or punch him hard<br/> And from him hide behind a curtain.”<br/> ― Alexander Theroux [<i>Ergänzung von</i> "N.R."]<br/><br/>Do I really have to say that again?<br/><br/>But, so, let’s look at what Steven Moore claims to be the stream of spawn flowing f</span>
  <span id="freeText7280071174583046832" style="display:none"><b>The Shandian Spawn</b><br /><br />“If on a friend’s bookshelf<br /> You cannot find Joyce or Sterne<br /> Cervantes, Rabelais, or Burton,<br /><br /> “[Gaddis or Gass, Pynchon or McElroy,<br /> David Foster Wallace, William T Vollmann,<br /> Alexander Theroux or Gilbert Sorrentino,]<br /><br /> “You are in danger, face the fact,<br /> So kick him first or punch him hard<br /> And from him hide behind a curtain.”<br /> ― Alexander Theroux [<i>Ergänzung von</i> "N.R."]<br /><br />Do I really have to say that again?<br /><br />But, so, let’s look at what Steven Moore claims to be the stream of spawn flowing forth from the narrative wealth of <i>The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman</i>.<br /><br />We begin at the very beginning, which is just after Sterne himself (this is not the proper way to begin ; one should begin at conception, back in Greece, Rome, China, India, Japan, etc, but that is what Moore’s Volume the First and much of Volume the Second were for) :: Voltaire’s <i>Potpourri</i> (1765), <i>The Man with Forty Crowns</i> (1768), and <i>Lord Chesterfield’s Ears</i> (1775) ; Diderot’s <i>Jacques the Fatalist and His Master</i> ; Xavier de Maistre’s <i>Voyage around My Room</i> ; some novels from the German likes of Wieland, Nicolai, Hippel, Wezel, Richter ; and the American novelist High Henry Brackenridge. <br /><br />So much for the 18th century. Lets’ continue into the 19th and 20th centuries using more or less Mr Moore’s words, because it is Mr Moore’s list and I am only reproducing it here because MORE PEOPLE NEED TO READ MORE BOOKS LIKE <b>THIS</b> KIND OF BOOK! You know, this kind of book tends to get BURIED. -- Also, one should read ALL of both of Moore’s novel-books because there are even MORE books found there that you’ve never heard of and with which you may find yourself IN LOVE.<br /><br />BEGIN QUOTATION OF STEVEN MOORE (notice the quotation marks) ::<br /><br />“Beginning in the 19th century, the trickle turned into a stream: the <i>Shandy</i> family genes can be detected in Charles Lucas’s <i>Infernal Quixote</i> (1801), Nicolai Wergeland’s <i>Petty Incidents in the Life of Haldor Smek</i> (1805-10), Washington Irving’s <i>History of New York</i> (1809), Ferenc Verseghy’s <i>Merry Life and Ridiculous Opinions of Gergely Kolomposi Szarvas</i> (1814-15), Thomas Love Peacock’s <i>Headlong Hall</i> (1815), Lord Byron’s verse-novel <i>Don Juan</i> (1818-23), which he called ‘a poetical <i>T Shandy</i>,’ E.T.A. Hoffmann’s <i>Life and Opinions of the Tomcat Murr</i> (1820-22), several of American John Neal’s novels (<i>Randolph</i>, <i>Errata</i>, <i>Authorship</i>), Yakov de Sanglen’s <i>Life and Opinions of a New Tristram</i> (1825), Charles Nodier’s <i>Story of the King of Bohemia and His Seven Castles</i> (1830), 19-year-old Karl Marx’s <i>Scorpion and Felix</i> (1837, unfortunately incomplete), Robert Southey’s <i>Doctor</i> (1834-47), Thomas Carlyle’s <i>Sartor Resartus</i> (1836), Nicolai Gogol’s ‘Nose’ (1836; Pushkin called Gogol ‘the Russian Sterne’), Søren Kierkegaard’s <i>Either-Or/Stages on Life’s Way</i> diptych (1843-45), Almedia Garrett’s <i>Travels in My Homeland</i> (1846), Herman Melville’s <i>Mardi</i> (1849) and <i>Moby-Dick</i> (1851), Lewis Carroll’s <i>Alice in Wonderland</i> (1865, 1871), Ippolito Nievo’s <i>Castle of Fratta</i> (1867), and earlier novels; Júlio Dinis’s <i>English Family</i> (1868), Carlo Dossi’s <i>Life of Alberto Pisani</i> (1870), Gustave Flaubert’s <i>Bouvard and Pécuchet</i> (1881), and in Joaquim Machado de Assis’s later novels. By the end of the century, Sterne’s spawn could be found throughout continental Europe, Scandinavia, and Russia.”<br /><br />[I’m still quoting Moore (notice the quotation marks) ; but here I’m gonna reformat into a columnar list, for read-abble’s-sake, since I can’t reproduce the typesetting on Moore’s page which is intended to intimate the shape of an ever-widening river.]<br /><br />“In the 20th century, the stream widened into a river, beginning with <br />Natsume Soseki’s <i>I Am a Cat</i>, and including <br />Miguel de Unamuno’s <i>Mist</i>, <br />Andrei Bely’s <i>Petersburg</i>, <br />James Joyce’s <i>Ulysses</i> and <i>Finnegans Wake</i> (he cited Sterne when describing the latter), <br />Vikto Shklovsky’s <i>Sentimental Journey</i>, <br />Italo Svevo’s <i>Confessions of Zeno</i>, <br />Andre Gide’s <i>Counterfeiters</i>, <br />Luigi Pirandello’s <i>One, No One, and One Hundred Thousand</i>, <br />Alfred Döblin’s <i>Berlin Alexanderplatz</i>, <br />Stanislaw Witkiewicz’s <i>Insatiability</i>,<br />Miklós Szentkuthy’s <i>Prae</i>,<br />John Dos Passos’s <i>USA</i>,<br />Juan Filly’s <i>Op Oloop</i> and <i>Faction</i>,<br />Philip Wylie’s <i>Finnley Wren</i> and <i>Opus 21</i>,<br />Witold Gombrowicz’s <i>Ferdydurke</i>,<br />Vladimir Nabokov’s <i>Real Life of Sebastian Knight</i>,<br />Flann O’Brien’s <i>At Swim-Two-Birds</i>,<br />Mikhail Bulgakov’s <i>Master and Margarita</i>,<br />Macedonio Fernandez’s <i>Museum of Eterna’s Novel</i>,<br />Kenneth Patchen’s <i>Journal of Albion Moonlight</i>,<br />Thomas Mann’s <i>Doctor Faustus</i>,<br />Felipe Alfau’s <i>Chromos</i>,<br />Louis Paul Boon’s <i>Summer in Termuren</i>,<br />Günter Grass’s <i>Tin Drum</i> and <i>Flounder</i>,<br />Jack Kerouac’s <i>Old Angel Midnight</i> (‘And Tristram Shraundy Shern, marvelous book’),<br />Julio Cortázar’s <i>Hopscotch</i>,<br />Guillermo Cabrera Infante’s <i>Three Trapped Tigers</i>,<br />Richard Brautigan’s <i>Trout Fishing in America</i>,<br />William H. Gass’s <i>Willie Masters’ Lonesome Wife</i> and <i>The Tunnel</i>,<br />Steve Katz’s <i>Exagggerations of Peter Prince</i>,<br />Venedikt Erofeev’s <i>Moscow to the End of the Line</i>,<br />Ronald sukenick’s <i>Up</i>,<br />Kurt Vonnegut’s <i>Slaughterhouse-Five</i>,<br />Donald Harington’s <i>Some Other Place. The Right Place.</i>,<br />Chandler Brossard’s <i>A Chimney Sweep Comes Clean</i>,<br />Severo Sarduy’s <i>Cobra</i>,<br />Thomas Pynchon’s <i>Gravity’s Rainbow</i>,<br />Augusto Roa Bastos’s <i>I the Supreme</i>,<br />José Lezama Lima’s <i>Paradiso</i>,<br />Raymond Federman’s <i>Take It or Leave It</i>,<br />Juan Goytisolo’s <i>Juan the Landless</i>,<br />Fernando del Paso’s <i>Palinuro of Mexico</i>,<br />Arno Schmidt’s <i>Evening Edged in Gold</i>,<br />Portuguese collaborators Manuel da Silva Ramos and Alface’s experimental novels, <br />David Markson’s <i>Springer’s Progress</i>,<br />Georges Perec’s <i>Life a User’s Manual</i>,<br />Ignácio de Loyola Brandão’s <i>Zero</i>,<br />Italo Calvino’s <i>If on a winter’s night a traveler</i>,<br />Alfredo Bryce Echenique’s <i>A World for Julius</i>,<br />Gilbert Sorrentino’s <i>Mulligan Stew</i>,<br />Alexander Theroux’s <i>Darconville’s Cat</i>,<br />D. Keith Mano’s <i>Take Five</i>,<br />Salman Rushdies’ <i>Midnight’s Children</i>,<br />Genichiro Takahashi’s <i>Sayonora, Gangsters</i>,<br />Julián Ríos’s <i>Larva</i>,<br />Aladair Gray’s <i>1982, Janine</i> and <i>Old Men in Love</i><br />Aldo Busi’s <i>Standard Life of an Ordinary Pantyhose Salesman</i>,<br />George Garrett’s <i>Poison Pen</i>,<br />Carlos Fuentes’s <i>Christopher Unborn</i>,<br />Jacques Roubaud’s <i>Great Fire of London</i>,<br />Fernando Arrabal’s <i>Extravagant Crusade of a Castrated Man in Love</i>,<br />Thomas McGonigle’s <i>Going to Patchogue</i>,<br />David Foster Wallace’s novels<br />and some of William T. Vollmann’s,<br />Héctor Abad Faciolince’s <i>Joy of Being Awake</i>,<br />Javier Marías <i>Dark Back of Time</i> (written after he had translated <i>Tristram Shandy</i> into Spanish),<br />Haruki Murakami’s <i>Wind-up Bird Chronicle</i>,<br />Matthew Remski’s <i>Silver</i>,<br />Walter Moer’s <i>13 1/2 Lives of Captain Bluebear</i>,<br />Joseph Heller’s <i>Portrait of an Artist, as an Old Man</i>,<br />several of Percival Everett’s novels,<br />Daniel Sada’s <i>Porque parece mentira la verdad nunca se sabe</i>,<br />Mark Z. Danielewski’s <i>House of Leaves</i>,<br />Robert Juan-Cantavella’s <i>Otro</i>,<br />Per Højholt’s <i>Aruicula</i>,<br />Robert Coover’s <i>Lucky Pierre</i>,<br />Steve Tomasula’s <i>VAS</i>,<br />Enrique Vila-Matas’s <i>Montano’s Malady</i>,<br />Jasper Fforde’s ffictions<br />Gordon Sheppard’s <i>Ha!</i>,<br />Paul Anderson’s <i>Hunger’s Brides</i>,<br />Adam Thirwell’s <i>Politics</i>,<br />Jeff VanderMeer’s <i>City of Saints and Madmen</i>,<br />James McCourt’s <i>Now Voyagers</i>,<br />Joshua Cohen’s <i>Cadenza for the Schneidermann Violin Concerto</i>,<br />Evan Dara’s <i>Easy Chain</i>,<br />Lee Henerson’s <i>Man Game</i>,<br />Benjamin Zucker’s talmudic trilogy,<br />Matthew Roberson’s <i>Impotent</i>,<br />John McGreal’s <i>Book of It</i>,<br />Lawrence Sutin’s <i>When to Go into the Water</i>,<br />Adam Levin’s <i>Instructions</i>,<br />Arthur Phillip’s <i>Tragedy of Arthur</i>,<br />Sergio De La Pava’s <i>Personae</i>,<br />Tom Carson’s <i>Daisy Buchanan’s Daughter</i>,<br />Jan Brandt’s <i>Gegen die Welt</i>,<br />Mark Leyner’s <i>Sugar Frosted Nutsack</i>,<br />Will Self’s <i>Umbrella</i>,<br />Chris Eaton’s <i>Chris Eaton</i> --”<br />[continues Moore] “-- I’ll stop there, for as Calvino wrote in 1981, <i>Tristram Shandy</i> is the ‘undoubted progenitor of all avant-garde novels of our century.’”<br /><br />Moore goes on to make a “three degrees of separation or fewer to Laurence Sterne” kind of a claim ; by way of such renown’d fictioneers as “Balzac, Dickens, Tolstoy, Eliot, and Twain.”<br /><br />BUT :: you’ll notice what is not on this list ; same thing that’s not on many such lists ; in fact, it is missing the same thing which is missing on most of my lists -- female authors. Let’s listen in on Moore’s footnote about his anomoly ::<br /><br />[I begin quoting Moore again ; notice the quotation marks] “That list, you’ve probably noticed, is a total sausage fest; the daughters of <i>Tristram Shandy</i> might include Djuna Barnes’s <i>Ryder</i>,<br />Virginia Woolf’s <i>Orlando</i>,<br />Brigid Brophy’s <i>In Transit</i>,<br />Julieta Campos’s <i>Fear of Losing Eurydice</i>,<br />Gabrielle Burton’s <i>Heartbreak Hotel</i>,<br />Jaimy Gordon’s <i>Shamp of the City-Solo</i>,<br />Janice Galloway’s <i>Trick Is to Keep Breathing</i> (‘This book resembles <i>Tristram Shandy</i> as rewritten by Sylvia Plath’ --NYTBR),<br />Sarah Schulman’s <i>Empathy</i>,<br />Jeanette Winterson’s <i>Gut Symmetries</i>,<br />Helen DeWitt’s <i>Last Samurai</i>,<br />Heather Woodbury’s <i>What Ever</i>,<br />Cintra Wilson’s <i>Colors Insulting to Nature</i>,<br />Vanessa Place’s <i>La Medusa</i>,<br />Nicola Barker’s <i>Darkmans</i>,<br />Emilie Autumn’s <i>Asylum for Wayward Victorian Girls</i>,<br />Carol Hart’s <i>History of the Novel in Ants</i>,<br />Sheila Heti’s <i>How Should a Person Be?</i>,<br />certain novels by Kathy Acker,<br />Christine Brooke-Rose,<br />Rikki Ducornet,<br />Thalia Field,<br />Xiaolu Guo,<br />Carole Maso,<br />Ali Smith, and<br />Aritha Van Herk,<br />and some formally innovative YA novels by the likes of <br />Susie Day,<br />E. Lockhart, and<br />Lauren Myracle. But Sterne’s cocktail of comic erudition, slap-and-tickle sexuality, bittersweet sentimentalism, and achronological form doesn’t seem to attract many women writers -- or women readers, according to Professor Elizabeth Terries. She says in her career she’s taught <i>Tristram Shandy</i> to nearly 500 female students, and estimates ‘not more than twenty enjoyed reading Sterne’s work or will ever return to it.’”<br /><br />So. <br /><br />The foregoing list -- which belongs to Mr Steven Moore and was originally published in his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17158246.The_Novel_An_Alternative_History__1600_1800" title="The Novel An Alternative History, 1600-1800 by Steven Moore" rel="nofollow noopener">The Novel: An Alternative History, 1600-1800</a> -- shall, in the near future, be incarnated yet again in the form of a Listopia list, curtesy of Friend Aubrey. I’ll link to it from here at that time.<br /><br />For a similar kind of Wonder=List (and much duplication), I’ve got a Rabelaisian list over there in my Rabelais Review. You won’t have difficulty finding it. It’s fantastic!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7280071174583046832" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_800351506" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/800351506" />
    <a title="Darwin8u" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u"><img alt="Darwin8u" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547440402p2/2294090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/800351506?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darwin8u" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darwin8u" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u">Darwin8u</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140242041" href="/book/show/118219.The_Life_and_Opinions_of_Tristram_Shandy_Gentleman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2013">2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=aere-perennius">aere-perennius</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer800351506" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8312104760716686842"><i>“Shall we for ever make new books, as apothecaries make new mixtures, by pouring only out of one vessel into another?”</i> <br/>― Laurence Sterne, The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1448682037i/17176006._SY540_.jpg' width='400' height='465' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><i>A POEM IN WHICH IS A CELEBRATION BY NEGATION<br/>or, a repartee on jeopardy.<br/><br/>If on a friend’s bookshelf<br/>You cannot find Joyce or Sterne<br/>Cervantes, Rabelais, or Burton,<br/><br/>You are in danger, face the fact,<br/>So kick him first or punch him hard<br/>And from him hide behind a curtain.</i><br/> ― Alexander Theroux*<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1448682188i/17176023.png' width='400' height='303' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I was (of course) destined t</span>
  <span id="freeText8312104760716686842" style="display:none"><i>“Shall we for ever make new books, as apothecaries make new mixtures, by pouring only out of one vessel into another?”</i> <br>― Laurence Sterne, The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1448682037i/17176006._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="465" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>A POEM IN WHICH IS A CELEBRATION BY NEGATION<br>or, a repartee on jeopardy.<br><br>If on a friend’s bookshelf<br>You cannot find Joyce or Sterne<br>Cervantes, Rabelais, or Burton,<br><br>You are in danger, face the fact,<br>So kick him first or punch him hard<br>And from him hide behind a curtain.</i><br> ― Alexander Theroux*<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1448682188i/17176023.png" width="400" height="303" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I was (of course) destined to love this book. Just look at my love for/on Montaigne, Cervantes &amp; Burton. J'adore big books full of absurdity and digressions and allusions. This is the ... THE ... grand-pappa of the modern novel; the paterfamilis of all things Shandy. <br><br>Looking into the black night after emerging with a book from my mother's womb, I dreamt of THIS book among the stars. Sterne's Tristam existed for me before I read it. It was like a song whose tune you hum in your head for years, before identifying the tune with an actual song. Tristram Shandy was playing in the background as I read Joyce, Nabokov, Kerouac, Vonnegut, Murakami, Pynchon, DFW, Rushdie, Woolf, etc. Hell, even Karl Marx loved this book. <br><br>But now, I find myself debating on whether I will be content with my Modern Library (Fokenflik intro and notes) version or if I need to go buy the <a href="http://www.visual-editions.com/our-books/tristram-shandy" rel="nofollow noopener">Visual Edition</a> or the <a href="http://www.english.ufl.edu/faculty/publications/2007fall/new_florida-sterne.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Florida Edition</a>. <br><br>IF this seems like an odd obsession after reading/finishing Tristram Shandy, perhaps you haven't read it. It just isn't one of those books you really escape from. I keep digressing back into the novel because you keep recognizing the novel in other novels and movies and people. I look at Mandelbrot sets and think THIS is Tristram Shandy with its digressions, repetitions, and spawn. I look at the endnotes of DFW and think, this IS a Shandian experiment. I look at Vonnegut's picture of an <a href="http://yourkidsnotgoingpro.files.wordpress.com/2009/06/vonnegut2yv1.jpg" rel="nofollow noopener">* asshole</a> (pg 81) in BreakFast of Champions and think: this is a Shandian experiment.<br><br>Sterne was postModern before postModern was cool. Reading Tristram Shandy is like discovering that someone in the 18th century had already built a working computer, but that all it did was spit out a long sequence of digressions. Anyway, my wife informed me that she loved just watching me read (so this is now a voyeur review) Sterne because I would spit, giggle, choke, and squirm every page. I would wiggle and twist as Sterne would allude to the classics and twist the logic and satirize everyone from Robert Burton to Jonathan Swift to William Warburton. I can't say this novel isn't appreciated. Those who have read it get it, but it isn't appreciated enough. I imagine it will be like discovering Frank Zappa in 200 years. A future me will be looking at old YouTube videos and will think GOD why didn't more people appreciate him?<br><br>* props to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/691208851" rel="nofollow noopener">Nathan N.R. Gaddis</a> for uncovering/exposing this poem.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8312104760716686842" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_150037939" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150037939" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/150037939?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780486456485" href="/book/show/76529.The_Life_and_Opinions_of_Tristram_Shandy_Gentleman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=exert-yourself">exert-yourself</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-5-stars">reviews-5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=sterne">sterne</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer150037939" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6424564120277842615"><b>Dedication</b><br/><br/>This was a re-read of a novel that I first read when I was about 14 and that has stayed fresh in my mind ever since.<br/><br/>It was recommended to me by my cricket coach and favourite teacher, John Carr, who taught me English for five years and cemented my passion for Literature in the early 70’s. His Master’s Thesis was on Evelyn Waugh’s <i>"Sword of Honour”</i> Trilogy (which I’ve also read and plan to re-read). <br/><br/>I was amused to learn from Steven Moore that one John Carr rushed out a fake version of </span>
  <span id="freeText6424564120277842615" style="display:none"><b>Dedication</b><br><br>This was a re-read of a novel that I first read when I was about 14 and that has stayed fresh in my mind ever since.<br><br>It was recommended to me by my cricket coach and favourite teacher, John Carr, who taught me English for five years and cemented my passion for Literature in the early 70’s. His Master’s Thesis was on Evelyn Waugh’s <i>"Sword of Honour”</i> Trilogy (which I’ve also read and plan to re-read). <br><br>I was amused to learn from Steven Moore that one John Carr rushed out a fake version of volume 3 of <i>"Tristram Shandy”</i> four months before Laurence Sterne had published his own version. Long live homage and fan fiction!<br><br>This review is dedicated to both John Carr’s, one a teacher and the other a shit stirrer!<br><br><b>"Let Me Go On, and Tell My Story My Own Way”</b><br><br>The version of the novel that I read was 528 pages long. Don’t be afraid of the perceived length. The chapters are short and easy to read, plus it’s a lot of fun, once you get into the rhythm of the writing. Like a slippery slide, the hardest part is getting on; the rest is all downhill.<br><br>If you read anything about <i>"Tristram Shandy”,</i> you’ll discover it is full of digressions. This is only partly true. The assessment assumes that there is a path from which the author departs. It’s probably more accurate to say that he never embarks on a set path in the first place.<br><br>If a line can be said to be the shortest distance between two points, Sterne never really sets out to get from A to B, or to do it efficiently or quickly. He simply sits down to tell his story his way, as if we readers were sitting across from him at a pub or smoking our pipes in front of a fireplace. He’s in no hurry, but equally importantly neither are we. He simply asks that we let him get on and tell his story his own way.<br><br>Left to his own devices, he is individualistic and unconventional, and so is his novel.<br><br><b>In Which the Author Turns a Story Into a Plot</b><br><br>Steven Moore differentiates between a story and a plot:<br><br><i>"The story consists of the events in a novel as they would occur in chronological order; the plot refers to the novelist’s particular arrangements of those events.”</i><br><br>While Moore identifies the three key elements of the story, I don’t think they’re particularly important. What is most appealing is the methodology Sterne uses to convert them into a plot. For me, the most interesting aspects of the novel are the self-referential discussions of the writing of the novel and the relationship between author, work and reader.<br><br>These aspects are pure metafiction, and you could argue that no author has bettered them, before or after.<br><br><b>The Beauty of the Line (or the Line of Beauty)</b><br><br>The prevailing view of a narrative in a traditional realistic novel is linear. In the interests of efficiency and speed (i.e., distance travelled divided by time), the plot can be described in terms of a straight line.<br><br>A straight line has a mathematical and a scientific significance. However, it also has a moral, creative and social significance. <br><br>A straight line does not deviate to the left or the right. If we don’t deviate, we stay on the straight and narrow. Christians say it is the right path or the path of the righteous. Cicero describes it as <i>"an emblem of moral rectitude”.</i><br><br>If the line is vertical, it is upright or virtuous. If something falls from its top to its bottom, it experiences a divine gravitational force. By extension, the righteous feel gravitas.<br><br>Etymologically, all of these words are related: straight, direct, erect, right, upright, rectitude, righteous. The physical qualities morph into the moral and from there (via recht) into the legal.<br><br>Just as the right-handed ostracise the left-handed, the straight ostracise the bent, the crooked, the digressive and the divergent.<br><br>It’s this that Sterne rebels against.<br><br>He never sets out to follow the straight and narrow. His goal, so long as his neck remains flexible, is to follow his nose and his gaze, wherever they might lead him. And where he goes, so does his tale. It’s our pleasure and privilege to accompany him. <br><br><b>The Life of Beauty</b><br><br>Sterne takes a straight line and bends or curves it. He makes it more curvaceous, until it is closer to a line of beauty in the sense meant by Hogarth in his <i>“Analysis of Beauty”.</i><br><br>To quote wiki:<br><br><i>"According to this theory, S-shaped curved lines signify liveliness and activity and excite the attention of the viewer as contrasted with straight lines, parallel lines, or right-angled intersecting lines, which signify stasis, death, or inanimate objects.”</i><br><br>Thus, Sterne’s aversion for a straight line reflects an attraction to vitality, motion and dynamism.<br><br><i>"Tristram Shandy”</i> is nothing if not about vitality.<br><br><br><i>
  <b>"So vary'd he, and of his tortuous train <br>Curl'd many a wanton wreath, in fight of Eve, <br>To lure her eye."</b>
</i> <br><br><b>Milton</b><br><br><br><b>Of Riddles and Mysteries</b><br><br>Sterne’s objection to the straight line is also an objection to the logical processes that appear to govern our understanding of the world.<br><br>He doesn’t necessarily come across as a mystic. However, it seems that we need at least intuition to experience and enjoy the best that the world has to offer:<br><br><i>"We live amongst riddles and mysteries - the most obvious things, which come in our way, have dark sides, which the quickest sight cannot penetrate into, and even the clearest and most exalted understandings amongst us find ourselves puzzled and at a loss in almost every cranny of nature's works.”</i><br><br>Sterne objects to the plain, the joyless, the boring, that which lacks interest:<br><br><i>"There is nothing more pleasing to a traveller, or more terrible to travel-writers, than a large rich plain...[that presents nothing to the eye, but one unvaried picture of plenty.]”</i><br><br> <b>Of Conquests and Concupiscence</b><br><br>While form might override content in <i>"Tristram Shandy”,</i> it does rear its head in the last trimester of the novel, when it becomes clear that the true concern of the characters, both male and female, is sex. They are, one and all, seeking <i>"something perhaps more than friendship, less than love,”</i> at least to start with.<br><br>In retrospect, much of the dialogue is just playful or flirtatious or <i>"talking bawdy”,</i> as was the case with Sterne’s predecessor, Rabelais. The ultimate goal, for a male, is to tempt a pretty woman <i>"into a conversation with a pinch of snuff”:</i><br><br><i>"Why could not a man sit down in the lap of content here, and dance and sing and say his prayers and go to heaven with this nut-brown maid?”</i><br><br>Ironically, this was in France, which elsewhere Sterne would describe as <i>"foutre-land”,</i> though I confess I can’t give an accurate contemporary translation of the term.<br><br>Love and lust and amours (in which the reader longs for uncle Toby to get his oats) consist of thrusts and parries, just as much as any military battle. Fortifications and defences are broken down. Seductions follow campaigns and sieges (if you’re lucky).<br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1403570289i/10111428._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="120" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br><b>Of the Love Between an Author and a Reader</b><br><br>So, ultimately, Sterne seems to argue, <i>"talking of love is making it.”</i> If so, then you might well agree, what’s the hurry?<br><br>One lover’s digression is another’s foreplay. The point is to be aligned, if not vertically, at least horizontally.<br><br>Equally, the process of writing and reading follows some of the rules of attraction and love, at least to the extent that it depends on good communication and the sharing of the creative burdens between the two participants:<br><br><i>"Writing, when properly managed, is but a different name for conversation…The truest respect which you can pay to the reader’s understanding, is to halve this matter amicably, and leave him something to imagine, in his turn, as well as yourself.”</i><br><br>Thus, when the pleasure is equally shared, it’s possible that Tristram wasn’t necessarily complaining when he moaned, <i>“the more I write, the more I shall have to write.”</i><br><br>Perhaps what he really meant was that, the more I love, the more I shall have to love.<br><br>If this sounds like a <i>"fertile fancy”</i> or mere exaggeration, then, like Sterne:<br><br><i>"I beg the reader will assist me here...”</i><br><br><br><br><b>SOUNDTRACK:</b><br><br><b>Van Morrison - "Help Me" (from the live album "It's Too Late to Stop Now")</b><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Kn3GWzaZiwk&amp;list=PL4D5FAA5C08445178&amp;index=11">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Kn3GW...</a><br><br><b>Van Morrison - "Help Me" (Live at Montreux Jazz, 2012)</b><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=As03J5KXqPY">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=As03J...</a><br><br><b>Sonny Boy Williamson - "Help Me"</b><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QPeP3M-NqFo">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QPeP3...</a><br><br><b>Christelle Berthon - "Help Me"</b><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-xePm6Ju6B8">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-xePm...</a><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.harmonica-online.com/artists/Berthon.htm">http://www.harmonica-online.com/artis...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="6424564120277842615" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
