<div id="review_623832019" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/623832019" />
    <a title="Alan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5650849-alan"><img alt="Alan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1310234599p2/5650849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/623832019?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alan" href="/user/show/5650849-alan">Alan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer623832019" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6948826268607797451">Sterne invented a certain kind of modernity--the sexually allusive, apparently offhand, discontinuous, immediate....His prose, often written under the burden of tuberculosis, and even the despair of his wife, achieves an appearance that is genial and carefree. Uncle Toby is one of the great characters in English fiction. <br/> Sterne confesses that the more he writes, the further behind in the story he gets. A wonderful concept, and true for an expansive mind like his. One can think of others for who</span>
  <span id="freeText6948826268607797451" style="display:none">Sterne invented a certain kind of modernity--the sexually allusive, apparently offhand, discontinuous, immediate....His prose, often written under the burden of tuberculosis, and even the despair of his wife, achieves an appearance that is genial and carefree. Uncle Toby is one of the great characters in English fiction. <br /> Sterne confesses that the more he writes, the further behind in the story he gets. A wonderful concept, and true for an expansive mind like his. One can think of others for whom this may also be true: Joyce, in particular. But possibly also TS Eliot. John Updike? Whitman? Carl Sandburg in his poetry, not in his Lincoln.<br /> Remarkably Sterne wrote a quarter millenium ago, yet his truest confreres are fairly recent. Perhaps more remarkably, Sterne set the standard for hilarity, an unsurpassed standard for clerical humor. Did I say that? Surely his humor is not clerical, yet he was a cleric. Just as surely, Robert Herrick's poetry, except in his Noble Numbers, is not clerical, though he was a cleric. And John Donne's poetry. <br /> Oh dear, we have stumbled on the English Conundrum. It is inconceivable for a priest--an Irish priest, an Italian priest--to have written Tristram Shandy. Even America, which also educated principally ministers, failed to produce clerically-educated, literary humor like Sterne's.<br /> A clergyman, Sterne certainly knew how seriousness was enhanced by stance and by robes. Robes can carry the cleric who "carries" (or wears) them. Thus, he defines seriousness, "Gravity [is] a mysterious carriage of the body to cover the defects of the mind."* Yorick says this proverb should be graven in gold. Robes add to the mysteriousness of the carriage, and the hiding of mental defect.<br /> * Probably adapted from LaRochefoucauld, Maximes, 257.<br /><br /> As a note, my undergrad Shakespeare teacher (38 plays in year course) Theodore Baird wrote an article on Sterne, "The time scheme of TS and a source" which may have been published in the Norton Critical TS. (Baird owned the only FL Wright house in New England, built in 1940 for $5,000. Still stands at 37 Shays Street, Amherst, MA.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="6948826268607797451" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_13446722" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13446722" />
    <a title="Melissa Rudder" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/824216-melissa-rudder"><img alt="Melissa Rudder" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1201213962p2/824216.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/13446722?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa Rudder" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa Rudder" href="/user/show/824216-melissa-rudder">Melissa Rudder</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/824216-melissa-rudder?shelf=master-s-exam">master-s-exam</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer13446722" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17796814888555590945">I am shocked at the drastic change of my opinion on <i>The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy</i> by Laurence Sterne. After I read it a mere three years ago, I swore I would take my MA Exam without rereading it to avoid undergoing such torture a second time. I gave it one star on goodreads. Having forgotten everything about the novel (aside from my distaste for it), I had to reread it for the exam. And I thoroughly enjoyed it. I wrote "ha!" in the margins more than I have in any other book. I laughed</span>
  <span id="freeText17796814888555590945" style="display:none">I am shocked at the drastic change of my opinion on <i>The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy</i> by Laurence Sterne. After I read it a mere three years ago, I swore I would take my MA Exam without rereading it to avoid undergoing such torture a second time. I gave it one star on goodreads. Having forgotten everything about the novel (aside from my distaste for it), I had to reread it for the exam. And I thoroughly enjoyed it. I wrote "ha!" in the margins more than I have in any other book. I laughed out loud. I read sections to people around me because I thought they were amusing. I put the book down hesitantly when I had to take a break from it. Very strange. <br /><br />I think my main problem with reading <i>Tristram Shandy</i> the first time was that I was looking for the linear plot that reached its climax in the third act and then gracefully fell to its denouement. Sterne wants to shake up the expected system—something rather ahead of its time in the 1760s. On my first reading, I rebelled against his diversionary tactics and tangents and dangerously thrust my nose into the book searching for the next big plot development. And that is not how you should read <i>Tristram Shandy</i>.<br /><br />If you're willing to sit back and let Sterne guide you through his “Cock and Bull” story of large attractive noses that (he swears!) are just noses, hobby-horses, hot chestnuts down the pants, do-it-yourself chapters, excommunication forms, unconventional circumcisions, a conscious narrator and constructed audience, swearing nuns, and marble pages, you are sure to have a blast. Just don’t fight it.<br /><br />Quotes! <br /><br />“Every time a man smiles,--but must more so, when he laughs… it adds something to this Fragment of Life.”<br /><br />“Therefore, my dear friend and companion, if you should think me somewhat sparing of my narrative on my first setting out,--bear with me,--ant let me go on, and tell my story my own way:--or if I should seem now and then to trifle up<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6288.The_Road" title="The Road by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">on the road</a>,--or should sometimes put on a fool’s cap with a bell to it, for a moment or two as we pass along,--don’t fly off,--but rather courteously give me credit for a little more wisdom than appears upon my outside;==and as we jog on, either laugh with me, or at me, or in short, do any thing,--only keep your temper.” <br /><br />“Digressions, incontestably, are the sunshine;--they are the life, the soul of reading:--take them out of this book for instance,--you might as well take the book along with them.”<br /><br />“Endless is the Search of Truth!” <br /><br />“Writing, when properly managed, (as you may be sure I think mine is) is but a different name for conversation.”<br /><br />“Every thin in this world… is bit with jest,--and has with in it, and instruction too,--if we can but find it out.” <br /><br />“Sciences may be learned by rote, but Wisdom not.”<br /><br />“There are a thousand unnoticed openings… which let a penetrating eye at once into a man’s soul… a man of sense does not lay down his hat in coming into a room,--or take it up in going out of it, but something escapes, which discovers him.” <br /><br />“’Tis an undercraft of authors to keep up a good understanding amongst words, as politicians do amongst men—not knowing how near they may be under a necessity of placing them to each other.”<br /><br />“Love, you see, is not so much a Sentiment as a Situation, into which a man enters.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="17796814888555590945" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_19628063" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19628063" />
    <a title="ba" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/171103-ba"><img alt="ba" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1183572657p2/171103.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19628063?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ba" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ba" href="/user/show/171103-ba">ba</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19628063" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6576977958330212318">To be honest, I never heard of this book before the film came out last year. My wife heard an NPR report on the film, and they used the terms Post-Modern and Unfilmable so many times that she knew I would be interested. We saw the film and liked it. I finally picked upthe book and read it, expecting a challenging work that would yield some intellectual dividends if I could just plow through it somehow. In actuality, the book was a very fun read. It did indeed have the foreshadowings of postmoder</span>
  <span id="freeText6576977958330212318" style="display:none">To be honest, I never heard of this book before the film came out last year. My wife heard an NPR report on the film, and they used the terms Post-Modern and Unfilmable so many times that she knew I would be interested. We saw the film and liked it. I finally picked upthe book and read it, expecting a challenging work that would yield some intellectual dividends if I could just plow through it somehow. In actuality, the book was a very fun read. It did indeed have the foreshadowings of postmodern flair. Of course where one would today find cinematic references, there were instead references to Voltaire, Cervantes and Shakespeare. Really, much of the book plays with the (then new) form of the novel, and questions what is writable and what isn't. The narrative, such as it is, unfolds as a narrator tangents around an autobiography, augmented by an "editor" in the form of footnotes, and sometimes inserted right into the text. The narrator addresses the reader directly, often anticipating the reader's objections and arguing his point. The footnoting at points is pre-reminiscent(?) of the hilarity of David Foster Wallace at times, and the tension between "narrator" and "editor" reminds me of Nabokov's unreliable narrators. The confused dialog evokes both Don DeLillo and Philip K. Dick. Whimsical typography and comically abbreviated chapters recall (precall?) Vonnegut. <br /><br />In conclusion, for obvious reasons, I'm going to try to make it through this entire review without mentioning an author that rhymes with Schmynchon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6576977958330212318" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_672943727" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/672943727" />
    <a title="Roy Lotz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz"><img alt="Roy Lotz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443324405p2/14046996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/672943727?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roy Lotz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roy Lotz" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz">Roy Lotz</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9789626349670" href="/book/show/6720023-tristram-shandy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=highly-recommended-favorites">highly-recommended-favorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer672943727" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8334767268193373765"><blockquote><i>Nothing odd will do long. Tristram Shandy did not last.</i><br/>—Samuel Johnson</blockquote><br/>I wonder if Laurence Sterne, duty bound as he was, him being the author, or at the least the transcriber of this tale—as sometimes stories seem to come from some higher power, and we merely jot them down—I wonder, I say, if he had duly considered what he was about when he birthed this work from his brain;—whether he well understood how much depended on the doing, as there is after all no telling how many people will come to re</span>
  <span id="freeText8334767268193373765" style="display:none"><blockquote><i>Nothing odd will do long. Tristram Shandy did not last.</i><br />—Samuel Johnson</blockquote><br />I wonder if Laurence Sterne, duty bound as he was, him being the author, or at the least the transcriber of this tale—as sometimes stories seem to come from some higher power, and we merely jot them down—I wonder, I say, if he had duly considered what he was about when he birthed this work from his brain;—whether he well understood how much depended on the doing, as there is after all no telling how many people will come to read whatever you set out to write (though of course you might make a reasonable guess);—therefore it seems irresponsible of an author, not to mention a bit immoral, or at least indecent, not to polish and edit and redact every sentence in order to conform with good taste and strong morals;—as, I repeat, you never know who will come to read it, how long it will survive, or how much of an influence you might have—therefore it is imperative, I say, absolutely imperative, to sculpt and craft the work, to ensure that no ill effect will come about, no harm will be done to posterity, and that you will lead all the world, for generations on down, with your strong example of tact, decency, and restraint—if it should so happen, as no doubt is an unlikely possibility, that your work will continue to be read after you depart from this blue-green planet.<br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_143096610" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143096610" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/143096610?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780956569202" href="/book/show/9524501-the-life-and-opinions-of-tristram-shandy-gentleman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=reading-through-history">reading-through-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=top-100">top-100</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=metafiction">metafiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=unreliable-narrators">unreliable-narrators</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=rth-lifetime">rth-lifetime</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer143096610" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12111790844855906857">"Read, read, read, read, my unlearned reader! Read...for without much reading, by which, your reverence knows, I mean much knowledge, you will no more be able to penetrate the meaning of my next marbled page (motly emblem of my work!) than the world with all its sagacity has been able to unravel the many opinions, transactions and truths which still lie mystically hid under the dark veil of the black one." (III.35)<br/><br/>There's the most-quoted bit from Tristram Shandy, which is full of references to o</span>
  <span id="freeText12111790844855906857" style="display:none">"Read, read, read, read, my unlearned reader! Read...for without much reading, by which, your reverence knows, I mean much knowledge, you will no more be able to penetrate the meaning of my next marbled page (motly emblem of my work!) than the world with all its sagacity has been able to unravel the many opinions, transactions and truths which still lie mystically hid under the dark veil of the black one." (III.35)<br><br>There's the most-quoted bit from Tristram Shandy, which is full of references to obscure works, works made up, works misquoted, and works wholly plagiarized. <br><br>Well, okay, Shandy is an experiment. Titularly the story of its narrator, it turns out to be something entirely different: a story about his uncle, his father, the passage of time, the difficulty of telling a story...noses...it's anything other than Tristram Shandy's story. It's been described as a perfect capture of the way the mind works: twisting back on itself, skipping, tangentializing. And yeah, that's how my mind works, too, and as far as that documentation goes, it's bravura. But isn't the point of writing a novel to concentrate your mind, to focus all those disparate thoughts into a coherent whole? If I wrote down my mind right now, I would tell you about this book, Eric B &amp; Rakim on my CD player, my dog snoring, my wife asleep, my left calf aching slightly, the wine in my mouth, I suspect this review doesn't make much sense, and not in an awesome post-modern way, my fingers are a little cold, I'm still puzzling about a dream I had last night in which I told my wife that while she was gone on a business trip I'd shovel out the eight inches of sand I'd covered the floor of our library with, which she's been surprisingly obliging about but I was starting to get the impression that enough is enough...<br><br>That's not a very good narrative, and even the most forgiving of Tristram Shandy's critics have admitted that it's not a page-turner. The word is self-indulgent.<br><br>Shandy belongs to the Quixotic tradition - not as in the word, but as in the talking about the Cervantick [sic] influence - and I love that genre. It's writing about writing, and I was hoping to love this book, and I was excited about lots of parts of Shandy. For example: the page following the quote that opens this review is marbled; it was different, then, in every edition of this book as it was originally published. That's <i>weird,</i> and not lamely weird. There's also a part where Sterne threatens to describe the widow Wadman and then just leaves the next page blank, so you can draw her yourself, "as like your mistress as you like - as unlike your wife as your conscience will let you." (VI.38)<br><br>And he leaves IV.24 out because, he says, he realized after writing it that it was so good it would throw the balance of the rest of the book off; it would make everything else seem worse by comparison. Again, that's a funny joke. But I found myself a little disappointed by IV.25, because unlike 24, it existed. And when one finds oneself wishing that all of the chapters of a book had been excluded, one has to admit that one may not be enjoying reading it.<br><br>Tristram Shandy is a clever book. It might even be a worthwhile book, if you're really interested in books. But it's a bitch to read. <br><br><b>Edition review</b><br>I originally read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6239154-the-life-and-opinions-of-tristram-shandy-gentleman" rel="nofollow noopener">the Oxford edition,</a> which is excellent, but I'm now the proud owner of this artsy Visual Edition, wooo! It's one of the very nicest books on my shelves. Here are some pics:<br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1427057971i/14170985._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>The famous marbled page, "motly emblem of my work" - a very strange interpretation<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1427057971i/14170986._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>The famous black page, which follows the sentence "Alas, poor Yorick!" and is always blacked out one way or another<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1427057971i/14170987._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>I don't even know what's going on here but it's very cool - those are sortof like wax drips, only visible when you angle the page to catch the light. I did my best.<br><br>It's a very pretty edition but it does not have any notes, so it's probably not a good choice for your first exposure to Tristra Shandy. Go with the Oxford instead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12111790844855906857" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1468593995" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1468593995" />
    <a title="Ronald Morton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35853560-ronald-morton"><img alt="Ronald Morton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573140627p2/35853560.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1468593995?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ronald Morton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ronald Morton" href="/user/show/35853560-ronald-morton">Ronald Morton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140242041" href="/book/show/118219.The_Life_and_Opinions_of_Tristram_Shandy_Gentleman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35853560-ronald-morton?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35853560-ronald-morton?shelf=half-of-1001-books">half-of-1001-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1468593995" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16389156478113415577">This book is amazing. I’m just going to stick that up here at the top, before I go off on a tangent, so it shows up for any of you browsing reviews attached to this book.<br/><br/>Books like this cause me a great deal of anxiety. I know that’s weird, so let me explain. I’ve been aware of Tristam Shandy for the majority of my reading life, though it was only in college (a decade or so ago) that I became a bit more aware of the general <i>gist</i> of the work, which at least placed it somewhere on my “to read” lis</span>
  <span id="freeText16389156478113415577" style="display:none">This book is amazing. I’m just going to stick that up here at the top, before I go off on a tangent, so it shows up for any of you browsing reviews attached to this book.<br /><br />Books like this cause me a great deal of anxiety. I know that’s weird, so let me explain. I’ve been aware of Tristam Shandy for the majority of my reading life, though it was only in college (a decade or so ago) that I became a bit more aware of the general <i>gist</i> of the work, which at least placed it somewhere on my “to read” list, though it was pretty low on the list due to a general ambivalence I have towards 18th century literature. In the last few years I’ve read enough praise for this work from various quarters that I finally decided to give it a spin (and, as I’m planning on starting <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20385881.Leg_Over_Leg_4_Volume_Set" title="Leg Over Leg 4-Volume Set by Ahmad Faris Al-Shidyaq" rel="nofollow noopener">Leg Over Leg: 4-Volume Set</a> in the next few weeks, which was described in some review I read as the Arabic Tristam Shandy, and I figured I should probably read the original). And holy shit it’s good. And, see, that’s where the anxiety comes in: <i>what other books do I have a lifelong awareness of, a small aversion to, that are also incredible and I’m missing?</i> It’s worrisome.<br /><br />This book is, with no real exaggeration, one of the funniest I’ve read. I laughed out loud frequently – not just at the cunning wordplay frequently on display, but even more than that, at the structure itself. The character of Tristam Shandy (as narrator, obviously) is one of the greatest literary creations I’ve encountered yet. This book feels centuries ahead of its time, and at times still feels forward thinking reading it today. The rabbit hole of digressions that this book takes in the first three volumes is both overwhelming and exhilarating.<br /><br />That’s not to say it doesn’t also feel archaic – it’s odd to me (and telling, I suppose, of the quality of many modern texts) that reading a book with a high level of erudition, classical education, and depth and breadth of knowledge automatically makes it feel aged, but there it is. Sterne’s writing is incredibly learned and nuanced, displaying a depth of both learning and wit, typically in equal measure.<br /><br />Also: good lord is this book dirty. Good for it. In fact, if you run into a section that has multiple annotations, odds are good Sterne is making a sex joke, and typically it’s a pretty funny one.<br /><br />I will say that I felt that the book does not maintain the same level of intensity, humor, or just plain batshit insanity in the second half as is present in the first half. And even then, the book as a whole is incredible, and worth the time and effort (it can be quite difficult to keep up with Sterne’s/Shandy’s narrative flow at times) to read this book. It is truly deserving of the incredibly high regard in which it is held.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16389156478113415577" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('EBZmb84wCIReNcW7PGzcVhUtSsU2KKLgB5FI0fZpPZYqMA+9dW7B7PkecdrLmdaJw3KtDfilLIdc7w5gOyrPDA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

In the words of one contemporary, it divided the world &quot;into two different Parties, Pamelists and Anti-pamelists,&quot; even eclipsing the sensational factional politics of the day. Preached for its morality, and denounced as pornography in disguise, it vividly describes a young servant\'s long resistance to the attempts of her predatory master to seduce her. Written in the voice of its low-born heroine, Pamela is not only a work of pioneering psychological complexity, but also a compelling and provocative study of power and its abuse. Based on the original text of 1740, from which Richardson later retreated in a series of defensive revisions, this edition makes available the version of Pamela that aroused such widespread       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_529243'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/529243.Clarissa_or_the_History_of_a_Young_Lady?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Clarissa, or, the History of a Young Lady<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/17505.Samuel_Richardson?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Samuel Richardson<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.37 avg rating &mdash; 7,822 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1748\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer5911766175383103340\">Pressured by her unscrupulous family to marry a wealthy man she detests, the young Clarissa Harlowe is tricked into fleeing with the witty and debonair Robert Lovelace and places herself under his protection. Lovelace, however, proves himself to be a<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText5911766175383103340\" style=\"display:none\">Pressured by her unscrupulous family to marry a wealthy man she detests, the young Clarissa Harlowe is tricked into fleeing with the witty and debonair Robert Lovelace and places herself under his protection. Lovelace, however, proves himself to be an untrustworthy rake whose vague promises of marriage are accompanied by unwelcome and increasingly brutal sexual advances. And yet, Clarissa finds his charm alluring, her scrupulous sense of virtue tinged with unconfessed desire. \n\nTold through a complex series of interweaving letters, &quot;Clarissa&quot; is a richly ambiguous study of a fatally attracted couple and a work of astonishing power and immediacy. A huge success when it first appeared in 1747, and translated into French and German, it remains one of the greatest of all European novels. His political work was tapering off at this point, due to the fall of both Whig and Tory party leaders with whom he had been associated (Robert Walpole was beginning his rise). Defoe was never fully at home with the Walpole group. Defoe\'s Whig views are nevertheless evident in the story of Moll. The novel\'s full title gives some insight into this and the outline of the plot: &quot;The Fortunes &amp; Misfortunes of the Famous Moll Flanders, &amp;c.           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>

</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3900824102780836955">Laurence Sterne was an Irish-born English novelist and an Anglican clergyman. He is best known for his novels <i>The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman,</i> and <i>A Sentimental Journey Through France and Italy</i>; but he also published many sermons, wrote memoirs, and was involved in local politics. Sterne died in London after years of fighting consumption (tuberculosis).<br/><br/>See also <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laurence_Sterne'>http://en.wikipe</a></span>
  <span id="freeText3900824102780836955" style="display:none">Laurence Sterne was an Irish-born English novelist and an Anglican clergyman. He is best known for his novels <i>The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman,</i> and <i>A Sentimental Journey Through France and Italy</i>; but he also published many sermons, wrote memoirs, and was involved in local politics. Sterne died in London after years of fighting consumption (tuberculosis).<br /><br />See also <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laurence_Sterne">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laurence...</a></span>
